                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adma.202407274')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="09b693b31c4c4e0f8c89e52000591a65">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3f048c36-6a77-49e0-a1e8-96e0075c2064'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:hongwei@suda.edu.cn"><span>hongwei@suda.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:jplang@suda.edu.cn"><span>jplang@suda.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Gu/Hongwei">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Braunstein/Pierre" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a7" data-db-target-for="a7" aria-controls="a7" aria-haspopup="true" id="a7_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Pierre Braunstein</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a7" aria-labelledby="a7_Ctrl" role="region" id="a7">
                     <p class="author-name">Pierre Braunstein</p>
                     <p>University of Strasbourg – CNRS, Institute of Chemistry (UMR 7177 CNRS), 4 rue Blaise Pascal-CS, Strasbourg, 67000 France</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Braunstein/Pierre">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Lang/Jian%E2%80%90Ping" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Jian-Ping Lang<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Jian-Ping Lang</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d91885905" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Carbon materials have great potential for applications in energy, biology, and environment due to their excellent chemical and physical properties. Their preparation by carbonization methods encounters limitations and the carbon loss during pyrolysis in the form of gaseous molecules results in low yield of carbon materials. Herein a low-energy (600&nbsp;°C) and high-yield (82&nbsp;wt.%) carbonization strategy is developed using liquid gallium-assisted pyrolysis of metal-organic frameworks (MOFs) affording the N-doped carbon nanotube (CNT) non-hollow frameworks encapsulating Co nanoparticles. The liquid gallium layer offers protection against air, promotes heat transfer, and limits the escape of small carbonaceous gaseous molecules, which greatly improve the yields of the pyrolysis reaction. Experimental and theoretical results reveal that the synergistic interaction between CNTs and N/O-containing groups gives a non-hollow framework composed of N/O-enriched and open CNTs (NOCNTF-15, 15 denotes the 15&nbsp;mm thickness of the liquid gallium layer during the pyrolysis) with high specific capacity (185&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and ultra-stable cyclability (stable operation at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 50&nbsp;°C for 20&nbsp;000 cycles). This study provides a unique approach to carbonization that facilitates the practical application of low-cost CNTs and other MOFs-derived carbon materials in high-performance sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407274-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407274-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Carbon materials, including carbon nanotubes (CNTs), are increasingly applied in biology, environment, and energy owing to their light weight, excellent chemical stability, diverse structures, high electrical conductivity, and high mechanical strength.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0001_R_d91885445e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#adma202407274-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0003_R_d91885445e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They are typically prepared by subjecting precursors, such as polymers,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0004_R_d91885445e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> small organic molecules,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0005_R_d91885445e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Sci. Adv.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ionic liquids,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0006_R_d91885445e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and metal-organic frameworks (MOFs)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0007_R_d91885445e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Innov.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to high-temperature carbonization under controlled atmosphere. However, organic compounds may generate abundant volatile carbonaceous molecules during pyrolysis that readily escape, resulting in low yield of carbon materials and high energy consumption. Such drawbacks limit their large-scale applications,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0008_R_d91885445e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> especially for highly valuable CNTs. Mai and Zhao's groups have improved the yield of CNTs by selecting MOFs with high crystallinity and reducing the pyrolysis rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0009_R_d91885445e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, Yu et&nbsp;al. increased the yield of carbon materials by transition metal-assisted carbonization of small organic molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0005_R_d91885445e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Sci. Adv.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite such progress, the inherent limitations of traditional carbonization methods still leave substantial room for further improvements of their yields.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0010_R_d91885445e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0011_R_d91885445e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing a simple and effective high yield pyrolysis method for the preparation of carbon materials, especially CNTs, remains a formidable challenge.</p>
         
         <p>It is known that gallium has many interesting applications due to its unique physical and easily alloying properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0012_R_d91885445e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Nano">12</a>-<a href="#adma202407274-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0014_R_d91885445e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Kalantar-Zadeh et&nbsp;al. used liquid gallium as a solvent to explore the morphology and component selection of metal crystallization in liquid gallium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0015_R_d91885445e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Science">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fu et&nbsp;al. found that the negative mixing enthalpy between liquid gallium and metal elements contributed to the formation of high-entropy alloy nanoparticles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0016_R_d91885445e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nature">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, Daeneke et&nbsp;al. utilized liquid metal as a reaction environment for the synthesis of low-dimensional oxide nanomaterials and succeeded in isolating extremely thin subnanometer layers,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0017_R_d91885445e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Science">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0018_R_d91885445e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a>-<a href="#adma202407274-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0022_R_d91885445e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Sci. Adv.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Herein, we exploit the fluidity, high density, and high thermal conductivity of liquid gallium as a pyrolysis environment for MOFs to increase the yield of carbon products. It consists of a simple, high-yield (82&nbsp;wt.%) and low-energy (600&nbsp;°C) pyrolysis strategy affording an N-doped CNT non-hollow framework encapsulating Co nanoparticles (Co@NCNTF-15, 15 denotes the thickness of the liquid gallium layer during the pyrolysis is 15&nbsp;mm) based on the use of liquid gallium as a sealing layer to assist the pyrolysis of the zeolitic imidazolate framework-67 (ZIF-67). Compared to other pyrolysis methods, liquid gallium-assisted pyrolysis does not require harsh conditions nor protective atmospheres. Crucial advantages of the liquid gallium layer are to offer protection against air and to confine the small carbonaceous molecules and limit their escape, thus ensuring a higher yield preparation of CNTs. Furthermore, we can control the diameter and crystallinity of the CNTs by adjusting the thickness of the liquid gallium layer. Subsequently, Co@NCNTF-15 is chemically modified to form a stable N/O-enriched and open CNT non-hollow framework (NOCNTF-15), which is shown to be an excellent anode material for remarkable high-rate and ultralong lifespan sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). The capacity of the NOCNTF-15 anode is maintained at 185&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 21&nbsp;700 cycles at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 25&nbsp;°C, and 61% capacity retention is achieved even after 20&nbsp;000 cycles at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 50&nbsp;°C. Furthermore, the full cell (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F||NOCNTF-15) is developed with a high average working voltage of 4&nbsp;V and a high energy density of 233&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations as well as ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) reveal the sodium storage mechanism involving C&nbsp;=&nbsp;O groups at the surface of the highly conductive CNTs and their role in enhancing the adsorption capacity of Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407274-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407274-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407274-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202407274-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The overall procedure for the preparation of NOCNTF-15 is presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0001">1</a>. First, highly crystalline ZIF-67 crystals with a dodecahedral structure and a size of ≈700&nbsp;nm (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information) were covered with a 15&nbsp;mm thick layer of liquid gallium (amounting to a pressure of ≈867.9&nbsp;Pa) and placed in a muffle furnace for pyrolysis at 600&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). During the pyrolysis process, 2-methylimidazole produced a large amount of H<sub>2</sub> and small carbonaceous gaseous molecules<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0009_R_d91885445e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> that were confined under the liquid gallium layer, while Co<sup>2+</sup> was reduced by the H<sub>2</sub> produced to catalytically active Co nanoparticles (Co NPs). The material obtained after complete pyrolysis had the shape of a dodecahedron, formed by N-doped CNTs encapsulating Co NPs at their tips (Co@NCNTF-15). Unlike hollow structures reported previously,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0009_R_d91885445e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">9</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0023_R_d91885445e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0024_R_d91885445e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Energy">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the CNTs grew both outside and inside the framework to form a non-hollow structure. Compared to other approaches,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0005_R_d91885445e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Sci. Adv.">5</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0006_R_d91885445e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">6</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0008_R_d91885445e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">8</a>-<a href="#adma202407274-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0011_R_d91885445e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0025_R_d91885445e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> our strategy only required a temperature of 600&nbsp;°C and afforded a higher yield (up to 82&nbsp;wt.%) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The fluidity and high density of the liquid gallium layer allowed excellent coverage of the ZIF-67 crystals, not only providing protection against air and preventing oxidation of the carbides, but also confining the small carbonaceous gaseous molecules generated by ligand pyrolysis inside the framework, thus maximizing the retention of the carbon source. We examined the pyrolysis of ZIF-67 in N<sub>2</sub> by thermogravimetry-derivative thermogravimetry (TG-DTG). The TG analysis was performed to check the changes in ZIF-67 mass during pyrolysis. The DTG analysis revealed the transition temperature points. The mass of ZIF-67 decayed rapidly at ≈530&nbsp;°C and a transition temperature point appeared in DTG during its pyrolysis under N<sub>2</sub>, indicating the melting and decomposition of the crystals of ZIF-67 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a>, Supporting Information). The TG curve showed a mass loss of 57.4&nbsp;wt.% of ZIF-67 at 600&nbsp;°C, indicating that a large number of volatiles were pyrolyzed and escaped as gases during the pyrolysis process. ZIF-67 is one kind of MOF formed by the coordination of Co<sup>2+</sup> with dimethylimidazole, where the Co-N bond length (1.976&nbsp;Å) is much longer than the C-C and C-N bond lengths, which led to the preferential breakage of the longer Co-N bonds in ZIF-67 during pyrolysis. It underwent the cleavage reaction of the ligand-branched methyl group and the imidazole ring itself during the pyrolysis process, and the main organic gaseous products were CH<sub>4</sub>, C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>N, and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0009_R_d91885445e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">9</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0026_R_d91885445e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">26</a>-<a href="#adma202407274-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0028_R_d91885445e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Today">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These small organic molecules can sublime directly during the traditional heating process, leading to a decrease in the yield of carbon products.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0005_R_d91885445e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Sci. Adv.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, McFarland et&nbsp;al. reported that a high-pressure environment formed by gallium-based liquid metals can effectively inhibit the escape of small carbonaceous gaseous molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0029_R_d91885445e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Science">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this work, we successfully demonstrated that these small carbonaceous gaseous molecules formed by the decomposition of ZIF-67 could be confined within the high-pressure environment generated by liquid gallium. These molecules were further deposited on the surface of reactive Co nanoparticles formed by Co<sup>2+</sup> reduction and catalyzed to form CNTs with a high yield of 82&nbsp;wt.% (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), which was much higher than the yield obtained by TG analysis (42.6&nbsp;wt.%).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0024_R_d91885445e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Energy">24</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0030_R_d91885445e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">30</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0031_R_d91885445e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Carbon">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the high thermal conductivity of the liquid gallium layer assisted efficient heat transfer within and between the ZIF-67 crystals during pyrolysis, allowing a lowering of the temperature required to produce CNTs and consequently of the energy consumption. After pyrolysis, the liquid gallium can be easily recovered for reuse, greatly reducing the preparation cost. Moreover, when the preparation was scaled up to the gram level (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), similar CNT frameworks were also obtained in 80.2&nbsp;wt.% yield. When ZIF-8@ZIF-67 was used as a precursor, similar CNT non-hollow frameworks were obtained in 74.5&nbsp;wt.% yield (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), highlighting the versatility of our pyrolysis method. Finally, when treated with HNO<sub>3</sub> (9&nbsp;M), the tips of the CNTs in Co@NCNTF-15 were opened by removal of Co NPs while oxygen-containing groups were introduced on the CNTs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), thereby producing a non-hollow framework composed of N/O-enriched (42.01 at%) and open CNTs (NOCNTF-15).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407274-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cf568b52-5131-4f54-a118-93aa3ec53dbb/adma202407274-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cf568b52-5131-4f54-a118-93aa3ec53dbb/adma202407274-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f716d8d9-2ca8-49cf-8289-c724e8d04d2a/adma202407274-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cf568b52-5131-4f54-a118-93aa3ec53dbb/adma202407274-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407274-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407274" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram showing the liquid gallium-assisted synthesis of NOCNTF-15 from ZIF-67.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The morphology and structure of Co@NCNTF-15 obtained after pyrolysis under a 15&nbsp;mm liquid gallium layer were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). After pyrolysis, the Co@NCNTF-15 retained the backbone morphology of the original ZIF-67 and many CNTs were grown on the surface of the framework (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0002">2a,b</a>). Its non-hollow structure was evidenced by TEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0002">2d,e</a>). The tips of the CNTs were filled with Co NPs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0002">2c</a>), which is consistent with the tip growth mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0031_R_d91885445e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Carbon">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, High resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) revealed that these thick multi-walled CNTs were highly crystalline, with a lattice stripe spacing of 0.36&nbsp;nm, corresponding to graphite (002) crystal plane.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0024_R_d91885445e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Energy">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The angle (<i>θ</i>) between the alignment direction of these graphite layers and the axial direction of the CNTs was ≈30° (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0002">2f</a>). As a result, the CNTs displayed more exposed edges and defects. To investigate the influence of the liquid gallium layer thickness on the pyrolysis products of ZIF-67, it was adjusted to 0&nbsp;mm (which represents pyrolysis of ZIF-67 under Ar atmosphere), 5&nbsp;mm and 25&nbsp;mm, respectively. The Co@NPCF was obtained by pyrolysis of ZIF-67 under Ar atmosphere in the absence of the gallium layer (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), the outward diffusion of the small carbonaceous gaseous molecules generated by the rapid decomposition of 2-methylimidazole resulted in the inward contraction of the main framework to form an N-doped porous hollow framework encapsulating Co NPs (Co@NPCF). Obviously, in this case, CNTs were not observed in the framework, which may be due to the rapid escape of the carbon source during pyrolysis, preventing the growth of the CNTs. This was corroborated by a much lower relative yield (45.5&nbsp;wt.%) compared to the results obtained under liquid gallium-assisted pyrolysis (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0031_R_d91885445e730" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Carbon">31</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0032_R_d91885445e730" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, since it is well known that ethanol can serve as a carbon source for the growth of CNTs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0033_R_d91885445e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ZIF-67 was mixed with ethanol and placed under an Ar atmosphere for pyrolysis. It can be clearly seen from Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) that under these conditions, CNTs can be grown (named ETCNT), thus confirming that the liquid gallium layer helped promote the formation of CNTs by limiting the diffusion of small carbonaceous gaseous molecules. Moreover, an increase in the thickness of the liquid gallium layer resulted in an increase of the outer CNTs diameter from ≈7&nbsp;nm in Co@NCNTF-5 to ≈32&nbsp;nm in Co@NCNTF-25 (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). It is noted that the orientation of the axial direction of the CNTs became parallel to the alignment direction of the graphite layer in the CNTs wall when the liquid gallium layer reached 25&nbsp;mm, indicating that the CNTs crystallinity also increased with the thickness of the liquid gallium layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0002">2f</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10f</a>, Supporting Information). N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption curves showed that the liquid gallium layer had an impact on the specific surface area of the pyrolysis products. The Co@NPCF obtained by pyrolysis under Ar atmosphere had a specific surface area of only 108.08&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> whereas that of Co@NCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15 and 25) obtained by liquid gallium-assisted pyrolysis was much higher (209.69, 244.98, and 209.14&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the analysis of the pore size distribution in these materials using the Barrett-Joyer-Halenda (BJH) method revealed that only mesopores were present in Co@NCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15 and 25). However, macropores were also present in Co@NPCF, which can be ascribed to the prevention of carbon source release by the liquid gallium layer (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407274-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9c19e50f-c8eb-469a-bb28-b2087a296f7a/adma202407274-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9c19e50f-c8eb-469a-bb28-b2087a296f7a/adma202407274-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4783eeff-642c-4b27-a36e-1ac811cfd55e/adma202407274-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9c19e50f-c8eb-469a-bb28-b2087a296f7a/adma202407274-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407274-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407274" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology and structural characterization of Co@NCNTF-15 (obtained using a 15&nbsp;mm liquid Ga layer) and NOCNTF-15 (obtained by HNO<sub>3</sub> treatment of Co@NCNTF-15). a,b) SEM. d,e) TEM. c,f) HRTEM images of Co@NCNTF-15. g–i) SEM images and j–l) TEM images of NOCNTF-15.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>After treatment with 9&nbsp;M HNO<sub>3</sub> at 70&nbsp;°C, the polyhedral morphology of Co@NCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15, 25) was retained (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0002">2g–l</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The SEM images of the frameworks broken by the acid treatment revealed that CNTs were densely grown inside the frameworks (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, Co NPs at the tips of CNTs in Co@NCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15 and 25) were etched, resulting in the opening of the CNTs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0002">2j–l</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c–d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14c–d</a>, Supporting Information). The crystallinity of the carbon materials was evidenced by powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0003">3a</a>), with a sharp peak at 26.1° corresponding to the (002) crystal plane of graphite in NOCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15, 25).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0024_R_d91885445e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Energy">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the peak of the graphite (002) crystal plane in NOPCF was broad and of low-intensity, which is typical for amorphous carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0034_R_d91885445e840" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Electrochim. Acta">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> No characteristic peak of metallic Co was found in the PXRD patterns of NOCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15, 25), consistent with the complete removal of the Co NPs by HNO<sub>3</sub> treatment. The Raman spectra of NOCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15 and 25) and NOPCF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0003">3b</a>) contain two typical peaks, the D peak at 1348&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and the G peak at 1593&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to disordered carbon and graphitic carbon in these materials, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0035_R_d91885445e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Lett.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Their ratio indicated the degree of carbon graphitization in the material, with a smaller value of I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> indicating a higher degree of graphitization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0036_R_d91885445e865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Eng. J.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ratio of the intensities of the D and G peaks in the Raman spectra indicated that the degree of graphitization of the CNTs gradually increased with the thickness of the liquid gallium increased from 5 to 25&nbsp;mm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). This phenomenon is similar to the increase in graphitization degree of carbon materials caused by increasing the carbonization temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0037_R_d91885445e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It may be ascribed to the additional pressure exerted on ZIF-67 by the overlying liquid gallium layer during pyrolysis (the pressure generated by a liquid gallium layer of 5, 15, and 25&nbsp;mm thickness being 289.3, 867.9, and 1446.8&nbsp;Pa, respectively). The O1s spectrum of NOCNTF-15 exhibited two peaks at ≈531.47&nbsp;eV and ≈532.96&nbsp;eV, readily assigned to C&nbsp;=&nbsp;O and C-OH, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0003">3c</a>; <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17d–f</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0038_R_d91885445e888" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High resolution N1s XPS spectrum revealed the presence of two types of nitrogen atoms in the NOCNTF-15: pyridinic N at ≈397.88&nbsp;eV and pyrrolic N at ≈399.43&nbsp;eV (Figure <a href="#adma202407274-fig-0003">3d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a–c</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0039_R_d91885445e900" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Carbon">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS survey spectra showed that the material mainly consisted of C, N, and O elements, without noticeable Co characteristic peaks, which was consistent with the PXRD results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, XPS was also used to analyze the N and O contents of the samples after HNO<sub>3</sub> treatment. The N content in NOCNTF-15 was 7.7 at% much higher than that in NOPCF (4.87 at%), indicating that the liquid gallium-assisted pyrolysis also effectively inhibited the volatilization of nitrogen contained in the precursor (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0003">3e</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The high resolution XPS spectra further confirmed that the C&nbsp;=&nbsp;O content in Co@NCNTF-15 was 15.55 at%, and the C-OH content was 1.47 at% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). However, NOCNTF-15 exhibited a C&nbsp;=&nbsp;O content of 25.62 at% and a C-OH content of 8.69 at%, suggesting that the oxygen content, especially in the form of C&nbsp;=&nbsp;O, increased after a high concentration HNO<sub>3</sub> treatment, which is the result of the oxidation of hydrocarbons or olefins leading to the formation of ketones (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0003">3f</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407274-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a0ba5c7c-08ba-47f7-8da3-debffb41aa16/adma202407274-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a0ba5c7c-08ba-47f7-8da3-debffb41aa16/adma202407274-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4b75dac9-b316-4634-9d6d-1b61b5fad3ee/adma202407274-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a0ba5c7c-08ba-47f7-8da3-debffb41aa16/adma202407274-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407274-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407274" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Compositional and structural data for the samples obtained by pyrolysis of ZIF-67 at different liquid gallium layer thicknesses and further HNO<sub>3</sub> treatment. a) PXRD patterns and b) Raman spectra of NOCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15 and 25) and NOPCF. c) N 1s and d) O 1s XPS spectra of NOCNTF-15. e) The total N, pyridinic N and pyrrolic N content and f) the total O, C&nbsp;=&nbsp;O and C-OH contents derived from XPS spectra (0&nbsp;mm is the pyrolysis of ZIF-67 under Ar atmosphere).</div>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202407274-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202407274-sec-0040-title">2.2 Application for SIB</h3>
            
            <p>Considering that the carbon-based products after liquid gallium-assisted pyrolysis and acid treatment not only display a unique structure consisting of CNTs but also contain a significant amount of N and O, we investigated Co@NCNTF-15, NOPCF and NOCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15, and 25) as working electrodes for SIBs to explore their sodium storage performances and mechanisms. Within a voltage range of 0.01 – 3.0&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), the CV analysis was conducted on the NOCNTF-15 electrode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. A peak appeared in the first cycle at 0.83&nbsp;V and subsequently disappeared (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0004">4a</a>), because of the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0040_R_d91885445e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the following two scans, the CV curves exhibited good overlap, indicating excellent reversibility in NOCNTF-15. Furthermore, the CV curves displayed a near rectangle shape, indicating that the NOCNTF-15 electrode primarily operated through a surface-controlled mechanism during sodium storage, indicating excellent rate performance of the NOCNTF-15 electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0041_R_d91885445e976" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The rate performances of the Co@NCNTF-15, NOPCF and NOCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15, 25) electrodes at different current densities, with NOCNTF-15 exhibiting the best performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0004">4b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). At current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the NOCNTF-15 electrode delivered discharge capacities of 371, 349, 337 322, 304, 279, and 253&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current density was reverted to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the NOCNTF-15 electrode rapidly recovered its capacity to 339&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and remained stable in the subsequent cycles. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0004">4c</a> displayed the galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curves of the NOCNTF-15 electrode at different currents. The NOCNTF-15 electrode exhibited a high initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of up to 84.1% at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the curve had a clearly dominant slope region, suggesting that sodiation/desodiation was mainly a pseudocapacitive process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0042_R_d91885445e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrochemical impedance spectrum (EIS) comparison of Co@NCNTF-15, NOPCF and NOCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15, 25) electrodes were tested before charging and discharging and their charge transfer resistances (R<sub>ct</sub>) were obtained based on the equiv. circuit to be 81.69, 139.6, 79.51, 63.48, and 67.08&nbsp;Ω, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a,b</a>, Supporting Information). It was obvious that compared to the Co@NCNTF-15, NOCNTF-5, NOCNTF-15, and NOCNTF-25 electrodes consisting of CNTs with high electrical conductivity, the NOPCF electrode consisting of amorphous carbon had a greater R<sub>ct</sub>. This was probably due to the cross-linking of CNTs that promoted electron transfer. In addition, the NOCNTF-15 electrode had the smallest R<sub>ct</sub> due to the high N and O contents which modified the electron distribution in carbon-based materials and effectively improved the conductivity of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0043_R_d91885445e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Small">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the EIS of the NOCNTF-15 electrode were tested at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for different number of cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21c</a>, Supporting Information). The R<sub>ct</sub> of the NOCNTF-15 electrode was 28.65&nbsp;Ω after the first charge/discharge, and the R<sub>ct</sub> value decreased to 15.25&nbsp;Ω after 20 cycles, indicating that the NOCNTF-15 electrode has a fast electron mobility and interfacial kinetics (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21d</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0009_R_d91885445e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">9</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0044_R_d91885445e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano-Micro Lett.">44</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0045_R_d91885445e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the NOCNTF-15 electrode also had excellent long-term stability. It retained a specific capacity of 349&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). Even after 480 cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible capacity remained at 285&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and its CE was ≈100% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0004">4d</a>). The GCD curves indicated that the shapes of the curves were basically the same at different cycles, further confirming the long cycling stability of the NOCNTF-15 electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). To further evaluate its cycling durability at high current densities, the battery was subjected to 21&nbsp;700 cycles at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0004">4g</a>). after 21&nbsp;700 cycles, the NOCNTF-15 electrode could still deliver an outstanding reversible capacity of 185&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention rate of 70% and an average capacity decay of only 0.0014% per cycle.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407274-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/de9f1041-5072-412e-b813-a59a1061f952/adma202407274-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/de9f1041-5072-412e-b813-a59a1061f952/adma202407274-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9d365441-b3f4-4d3d-8d16-08d6a4bde225/adma202407274-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/de9f1041-5072-412e-b813-a59a1061f952/adma202407274-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407274-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407274" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium storage performance of NOCNTF-15 as an anode in one sodium-ion half battery at 25&nbsp;°C. a) CV plot of the NOCNTF-15 electrode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Rate performance of NOPCF and NOCNTF-x (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;5, 15, 25) at different current densities. c) The galvanostatic discharge-charge curves of the NOCNTF-15 electrode at different current densities. d) Cycling performance of the NOCNTF-15 electrode at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Comparison of the rate performances and f) cycling performances between the NOCNTF-15 electrode and other known carbon-based electrodes for sodium-ion half batteries. g) Long cycling performance of the NOCNTF-15 electrode at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>When the testing temperature was increased to 50&nbsp;°C, the rate performance of NOCNTF-15 electrode remained excellent. The NOCNTF-15 electrode delivered the specific capacities at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> were 342, 297, 250.7, 213.7, 180, 134, and 100&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the NOCNTF-15 electrode still exhibited an excellent long cycle life attributed to the novel stable structure of the NOCNTF-15. After 1&nbsp;000 cycles at 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible capacity retained at ≈82&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with an average capacity decay of only 0.043% per cycle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24c,d</a>, Supporting Information). Even at a high current density of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, this electrode could still provide a reversible specific capacity of 39&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after continuously charge/discharge for 20&nbsp;000 cycles and a capacity retention rate of 61% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24e</a>, Supporting Information). Clearly, the superior rate performance and long life-time of the NOCNTF-15 electrode outperformed those of previously reported carbon-based SIBs anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0004">4e,f</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The morphology and structure of this electrode after 21&nbsp;700 cycles at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> were examined to further explore the ultra-long cycling stability of the NOCNTF-15 electrode. Even after 21&nbsp;700 cycles, it can be clearly observed from the macroscopic structure that its surface remained intact, and there was no obvious pulverization or peeling of the electrode material (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25a</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, its microscopic structure showed that NOCNTF-15 retained its original non-hollow structure formed by opened CNTs crosslinking, indicating good stability and resistance to repeated sodiation/desodiation, resulting in an excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25b–f</a>, Supporting Information). In summary, the stable framework assembled from opened CNTs from inside to outside provided fast pathways for electron transport, minimizing electrical contact losses and resulting in exceptional rate performance and long life-time.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0046_R_d91885445e1134" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the high N/O content, especially the C&nbsp;=&nbsp;O groups exposed on the surface of CNTs, helped enhance Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption/desorption, leading to high Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407274-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0037_R_d91885445e1144" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#adma202407274-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407274-bib-0042_R_d91885445e1144" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To evaluate the potential of the NOCNTF-15 electrode for practical applications, a pre-sodiumated NOCNTF-15 electrode was used as the anode and paired with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF) cathode to assemble a sodium-ion full cell, denoted as NVPOF||NOCNTF-15, and tested in a voltage window of 1.25–4.3&nbsp;V (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0005">5a</a>). The micrometer-sized Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F particles with high crystallinity exhibited high charge/discharge plateaus at 4.01 and 3.6&nbsp;V in a half cell, and at the current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the NVPOF electrode could offer a specific capacity of up to 127&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the discharge specific capacities of the NVPOF and NOCNTF-15 electrodes at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the weight ratio of NOCNTF-15 and NVPOF was adjusted to ≈1:4.5. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0005">5b</a> displayed the specific capacities of this battery at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which are 547, 440, 393, 372, 309, 202, and 124&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (based on the active mass of the anode material). The NVPOF||NOCNTF-15 full cell also exhibited an average working voltage of 4&nbsp;V, corresponding to an energy density of 233&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total active mass of both electrodes) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0005">5e</a>). Life cycle and fast charging feature, as two of the important indicators to evaluate the value of batteries for practical applications, we tested for the NVPOF||NOCNTF-15 full cell at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. After 500 cycles at a current density of 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, it could still provide a reversible specific capacity of 83&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention rate of up to 86%, and after 1&nbsp;000 cycles, the capacity retention rate still reached 76% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0005">5f</a>). Even at a higher current density of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, this full cell still had a high-capacity retention of 82% and 77% after 6&nbsp;000 cycles and 12&nbsp;000 cycles, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0005">5g</a>). A more comprehensive assessment of the NVPOF||NOCNTF-15 full cell performance was provided by five key metrics presented in a radar chart (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0005">5c</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The NVPOF||NOCNTF-15 full cell occupied the largest area, highlighting its best overall performance compared to those of known sodium-ion full batteries. Furthermore, this full cell could power a light-emitting diode (LED) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407274-fig-0005">5d</a>), demonstrating its practical potential for energy supply.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202407274-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c9d35509-5fa4-4808-8386-be97ce6e4ba3/adma202407274-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c9d35509-5fa4-4808-8386-be97ce6e4ba3/adma202407274-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7f4468aa-8206-46c2-93ea-455a8caa059c/adma202407274-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c9d35509-5fa4-4808-8386-be97ce6e4ba3/adma202407274-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202407274-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202407274" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium storage performance of NOCNTF-15 as an anode in one sodium-ion full cell. a) Schematic diagram of the NVPOF||NOCNTF-15 full cell. b) Rate performance of NVPOF||NOCNTF-15 full cell at different current densities. c) Radar chart showing the overall performance comparison of the present work with the known sodium-ion full cells. d) A digital photo showing lighting up one LED bulb by a NVPOF||NOCNTF-15 full cell. e) The 1st – 5th charge/discharge curves of the NVPOF||NOCNTF-15 full-cell between 1.25–4.3&nbsp;V at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Long-term cycling stability at f) 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and g) 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of the NVPOF||NOCNTF-15 full cell.</div>
